<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC) secretariat, the overwhelming direct cause of deforestation is agriculture. <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Subsistence farming">Subsistence farming</a> is responsible for 48% of deforestation; <a href="/wiki/Commercial_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Commercial agriculture">commercial agriculture</a> is responsible for 32%; <a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">logging</a> is responsible for 14%, and fuel wood removals make up 5%.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Experts do not agree on whether industrial logging is an important contributor to global deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-causesof_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-causesof-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Some argue that poor people are more likely to clear forest because they have no alternatives, others that the poor lack the ability to pay for the materials and labour needed to clear forest.<sup id="cite_ref-causesof_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-causesof-13">[13]</a></sup> One study found that population increases due to high fertility rates were a primary driver of tropical deforestation in only 8% of cases.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other causes of contemporary deforestation may include <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption</a> of government institutions,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/International_inequality" title="International inequality">inequitable distribution of wealth and power</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a><sup id="cite_ref-population1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-population1-19">[19]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-r1_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r1-21">[21]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">Globalization</a> is often viewed as another root cause of deforestation,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> though there are cases in which the impacts of globalization (new ﬂows of labor, capital, commodities, and ideas) have promoted localized forest recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Riau_deforestation_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Riau_deforestation_2006.jpg/220px-Riau_deforestation_2006.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Riau_deforestation_2006.jpg/330px-Riau_deforestation_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Riau_deforestation_2006.jpg/440px-Riau_deforestation_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3406" data-file-height="2232" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Riau_deforestation_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The last batch of sawnwood from the <a href="/wiki/Peat_swamp_forest" title="Peat swamp forest">peat forest</a> in Indragiri Hulu, Sumatra, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Indonesia" title="Deforestation in Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. Deforestation for <a href="/wiki/Elaeis_guineensis" title="Elaeis guineensis">oil palm</a> plantation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2000 the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO) found that "the role of population dynamics in a local setting may vary from decisive to negligible," and that deforestation can result from "a combination of population pressure and stagnating economic, social and technological conditions."<sup id="cite_ref-population1_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-population1-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The degradation of forest ecosystems has also been traced to economic incentives that make forest conversion appear more profitable than forest conservation.<sup id="cite_ref-economicvalue_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicvalue-26">[26]</a></sup> Many important forest functions have no markets, and hence, no economic value that is readily apparent to the forests' owners or the communities that rely on forests for their well-being.<sup id="cite_ref-economicvalue_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicvalue-26">[26]</a></sup> From the perspective of the developing world, the benefits of forest as carbon sinks or biodiversity reserves go primarily to richer developed nations and there is insufficient compensation for these services. Developing countries feel that some countries in the developed world, such as the United States of America, cut down their forests centuries ago and benefited economically from this deforestation, and that it is hypocritical to deny developing countries the same opportunities, i.e. that the poor shouldn't have to bear the cost of preservation when the rich created the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-costarica_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-costarica-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some commentators have noted a shift in the drivers of deforestation over the past 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Whereas deforestation was primarily driven by subsistence activities and government-sponsored development projects like <a href="/wiki/Transmigration_program" title="Transmigration program">transmigration</a> in countries like <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a> in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, and so on, during the late 19th century and the earlier half of the 20th century, by the 1990s the majority of deforestation was caused by industrial factors, including extractive industries, large-scale cattle ranching, and extensive agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-rud_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rud-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_with_deforestation">Problems with deforestation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Problems with deforestation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric">Atmospheric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Atmospheric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Manantenina_bushfire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Manantenina_bushfire.jpg/220px-Manantenina_bushfire.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Manantenina_bushfire.jpg/330px-Manantenina_bushfire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Manantenina_bushfire.jpg/440px-Manantenina_bushfire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Manantenina_bushfire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illegal slash and burn practice in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, 2010</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Deforestation is ongoing and is shaping <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deforestation_and_climate_change" title="Deforestation and climate change">Deforestation is a contributor to global warming</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fearnidel_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fearnidel-35">[35]</a></sup> and is often cited as one of the major causes of the enhanced <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>. Tropical deforestation is responsible for approximately 20% of world greenhouse gas emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> deforestation, mainly in tropical areas, could account for up to one-third of total <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_deforestation_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_deforestation-37">[37]</a></sup> But recent calculations suggest that carbon dioxide emissions from deforestation and forest degradation (excluding <a href="/wiki/Peatland" class="mw-redirect" title="Peatland">peatland</a> emissions) contribute about 12% of total anthropogenic carbon dioxide emissions with a range from 6 to 17%.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Deforestation causes carbon dioxide to linger in the atmosphere. As carbon dioxide accrues, it produces a layer in the atmosphere that traps radiation from the sun. The radiation converts to heat which causes global warming, which is better known as the greenhouse effect.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Plants remove <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a> during the process of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, but release some carbon dioxide back into the atmosphere during normal respiration. Only when actively growing can a tree or forest remove carbon, by storing it in plant tissues. Both the decay and burning of wood releases much of this stored carbon back to the atmosphere. In order for forests to take up carbon, there must be a net accumulation of wood. One way is for the wood to be harvested and turned into long-lived products, with new young trees replacing them.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Deforestation may also cause carbon stores held in soil to be released. Forests can be either sinks or sources depending upon environmental circumstances. Mature forests alternate between being net sinks and net sources of carbon dioxide (see <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_sink" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide sink">carbon dioxide sink</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>).</p>
<p>In deforested areas, the land heats up faster and reaches a higher temperature, leading to localized upward motions that enhance the formation of clouds and ultimately produce more rainfall.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> However, according to the Geophysical Fluid Dynamics Laboratory, the models used to investigate remote responses to tropical deforestation showed a broad but mild temperature increase all through the tropical atmosphere. The model predicted &lt;0.2&#160;°C warming for upper air at 700 mb and 500 mb. However, the model shows no significant changes in other areas besides the Tropics. Though the model showed no significant changes to the climate in areas other than the Tropics, this may not be the case since the model has possible errors and the results are never absolutely definite.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SouthEast_Asia_fires_Oct_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/SouthEast_Asia_fires_Oct_2006.jpg/220px-SouthEast_Asia_fires_Oct_2006.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/SouthEast_Asia_fires_Oct_2006.jpg/330px-SouthEast_Asia_fires_Oct_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/SouthEast_Asia_fires_Oct_2006.jpg/440px-SouthEast_Asia_fires_Oct_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7200" data-file-height="4800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SouthEast_Asia_fires_Oct_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Borneo" title="Deforestation in Borneo">Fires on Borneo and Sumatra</a>, 2006. People use <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> deforestation to clear land for agriculture.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Reducing_emissions_from_deforestation_and_forest_degradation" title="Reducing emissions from deforestation and forest degradation">Reducing emissions from deforestation and forest degradation (REDD)</a> in developing countries has emerged as a new potential to complement ongoing climate policies. The idea consists in providing financial compensations for the reduction of greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from deforestation and forest degradation".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rainforests are widely believed by laymen to contribute a significant amount of the world's oxygen,<sup id="cite_ref-timesonline.co.uk_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline.co.uk-44">[44]</a></sup> although it is now accepted by scientists that rainforests contribute little net <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a> and deforestation has only a minor effect on atmospheric oxygen levels.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> However, the incineration and burning of forest plants to clear land releases large amounts of CO<sub>2</sub>, which contributes to global warming.<sup id="cite_ref-Fearnidel_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fearnidel-35">[35]</a></sup> Scientists also state that tropical deforestation releases 1.5 billion tons of carbon each year into the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrological">Hydrological</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hydrological">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> is also affected by deforestation. Trees extract <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> through their roots and release it into the atmosphere. When part of a forest is removed, the trees no longer transpire this water, resulting in a much <a href="/wiki/Dry_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Dry climate">drier climate</a>. Deforestation reduces the content of water in the soil and groundwater as well as atmospheric moisture. The dry soil leads to lower water intake for the trees to extract.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Deforestation reduces soil cohesion, so that <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">erosion</a>, flooding and <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a> ensue.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shrinking forest cover lessens the landscape's capacity to intercept, retain and <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpire</a> precipitation. Instead of trapping precipitation, which then percolates to groundwater systems, deforested areas become sources of surface water runoff, which moves much faster than subsurface flows. Forests return most of the water that falls as precipitation to the atmosphere by transpiration. In contrast, when an area is deforested, almost all precipitation is lost as run-off.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> That quicker transport of surface water can translate into <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash flooding</a> and more localized floods than would occur with the forest cover. Deforestation also contributes to decreased <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>, which lessens atmospheric moisture which in some cases affects precipitation levels downwind from the deforested area, as water is not recycled to downwind forests, but is lost in runoff and returns directly to the oceans. According to one study, in deforested north and northwest China, the average annual precipitation decreased by one third between the 1950s and the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trees, and plants in general, affect the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> significantly:</p>
<ul>
<li>their canopies intercept a proportion of <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>, which is then evaporated back to the atmosphere (<a href="/wiki/Interception_(water)" title="Interception (water)">canopy interception</a>);</li>
<li>their litter, stems and trunks slow down <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a>;</li>
<li>their roots create <a href="/wiki/Macropore" title="Macropore">macropores</a> – large conduits – in the soil that increase <a href="/wiki/Infiltration_(hydrology)" title="Infiltration (hydrology)">infiltration</a> of water;</li>
<li>they contribute to terrestrial evaporation and reduce <a href="/wiki/Water_content" title="Water content">soil moisture</a> via <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>;</li>
<li>their <a href="/wiki/Plant_litter" title="Plant litter">litter</a> and other organic residue change soil properties that affect the capacity of soil to store water.</li>
<li>their leaves control the <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a> of the atmosphere by <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiring</a>. 99% of the water absorbed by the roots moves up to the leaves and is transpired.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>As a result, the presence or absence of trees can change the quantity of water on the surface, in the soil or groundwater, or in the atmosphere. This in turn changes erosion rates and the availability of water for either ecosystem functions or human services.</p>
<p>The forest may have little impact on flooding in the case of large rainfall events, which overwhelm the storage capacity of forest soil if the soils are at or close to saturation.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">Tropical rainforests</a> produce about 30% of our planet's <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-timesonline.co.uk_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline.co.uk-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil">Soil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Soil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/350px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg" width="350" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/525px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/700px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2544" data-file-height="1696" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Deforestation for the use of <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Brazil" title="Deforestation in Brazil">Brazilian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>. The hill depicted is Morro da Covanca, in <a href="/wiki/Jacarepagu%C3%A1" title="Jacarepaguá">Jacarepaguá</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Undisturbed forests have a very low rate of <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> loss (<a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>), approximately 2 metric <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">tons</a> per square kilometer (6 short tons per square mile).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Deforestation generally increases rates of soil loss, by increasing the amount of <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a> and reducing the protection of the soil from tree litter. This can be an advantage in excessively leached tropical rain forest soils. Forestry operations themselves also increase erosion through the <a href="/wiki/Road_building" class="mw-redirect" title="Road building">development of</a> (<a href="/wiki/Forest_road" title="Forest road">forest</a>) <a href="/wiki/Road" title="Road">roads</a> and the use of mechanized equipment.</p>
<p>China's <a href="/wiki/Loess_Plateau" title="Loess Plateau">Loess Plateau</a> was cleared of forest millennia ago. Since then it has been eroding, creating dramatic incised valleys, and providing the sediment that gives the Yellow River its yellow color and that causes the flooding of the river in the lower reaches (hence the river's nickname 'China's sorrow').</p>
<p>Removal of trees does not always increase erosion rates. In certain regions of southwest US, shrubs and trees have been encroaching on <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a>. The trees themselves enhance the loss of grass between tree canopies. The bare intercanopy areas become highly erodible. The US Forest Service, in Bandelier National Monument for example, is studying how to restore the former ecosystem, and reduce erosion, by removing the trees.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tree_root" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree root">Tree roots</a> bind soil together, and if the soil is sufficiently shallow they act to keep the soil in place by also binding with underlying <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a>. Tree removal on steep slopes with shallow soil thus increases the risk of <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a>, which can threaten people living nearby.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Deforestation on a human scale results in decline in <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and on a natural global scale is known to cause the extinction of many species.<sup id="cite_ref-Benton_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benton-6">[6]</a></sup>{ The removal or destruction of areas of forest cover has resulted in a degraded environment with reduced <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-r1_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r1-21">[21]</a></sup> Forests support biodiversity, providing habitat for <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">wildlife</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> moreover, forests foster <a href="/wiki/Medicinal_plants" title="Medicinal plants">medicinal conservation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> With forest biotopes being irreplaceable source of new drugs (such as <a href="/wiki/Paclitaxel" title="Paclitaxel">taxol</a>), deforestation can destroy <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> variations (such as crop resistance) irretrievably.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Illegal_export_of_rosewood_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Illegal_export_of_rosewood_001.jpg/220px-Illegal_export_of_rosewood_001.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Illegal_export_of_rosewood_001.jpg/330px-Illegal_export_of_rosewood_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Illegal_export_of_rosewood_001.jpg/440px-Illegal_export_of_rosewood_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Illegal_export_of_rosewood_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Illegal_logging_in_Madagascar" title="Illegal logging in Madagascar">Illegal logging in Madagascar</a>. In 2009, the vast majority of the illegally obtained <a href="/wiki/Rosewood" title="Rosewood">rosewood</a> was exported to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the tropical rainforests are the most diverse <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> on Earth<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and about 80% of the world's known <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> could be found in tropical rainforests,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> removal or destruction of significant areas of forest cover has resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Soil_degradation" class="mw-redirect" title="Soil degradation">degraded</a><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> environment with reduced biodiversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Benton_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benton-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> A study in <a href="/wiki/Rond%C3%B4nia" title="Rondônia">Rondônia</a>, Brazil, has shown that deforestation also removes the microbial community which is involved in the recycling of nutrients, the production of clean water and the removal of pollutants.<sup id="cite_ref-ro_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ro-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been estimated that we are losing 137 plant, animal and insect species every single day due to rainforest deforestation, which equates to 50,000 species a year.<sup id="cite_ref-rain-tree.com_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rain-tree.com-65">[65]</a></sup> Others state that tropical rainforest deforestation is contributing to the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Holocene_mass_extinction" class="mw-redirect" title="Holocene mass extinction">Holocene mass extinction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The known extinction rates from deforestation rates are very low, approximately 1 species per year from mammals and birds which extrapolates to approximately 23,000 species per year for all species. Predictions have been made that more than 40% of the animal and <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">plant species</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> could be wiped out in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Such predictions were called into question by 1995 data that show that within regions of Southeast Asia much of the original forest has been converted to monospecific plantations, but that potentially endangered species are few and tree flora remains widespread and stable.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scientific understanding of the process of extinction is insufficient to accurately make predictions about the impact of deforestation on biodiversity.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Most predictions of forestry related biodiversity loss are based on species-area models, with an underlying assumption that as the forest declines species diversity will decline similarly.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-71">[71]</a></sup> However, many such models have been proven to be wrong and loss of habitat does not necessarily lead to large scale loss of species.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-71">[71]</a></sup> Species-area models are known to overpredict the number of species known to be threatened in areas where actual deforestation is ongoing, and greatly overpredict the number of threatened species that are widespread.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>A recent study of the Brazilian Amazon predicts that despite a lack of extinctions thus far, up to 90 percent of predicted extinctions will finally occur in the next 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">Economic impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Economic impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Damage to forests and other aspects of nature could halve <a href="/wiki/Living_standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Living standard">living standards</a> for the world's <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poor</a> and reduce global <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a> by about 7% by 2050, a report concluded at the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a> (CBD) meeting in Bonn in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Historically, utilization of forest products, including timber and fuel wood, has played a key role in human societies, comparable to the roles of water and cultivable land. Today, developed countries continue to utilize timber for building houses, and wood pulp for <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>. In developing countries almost three billion people rely on wood for heating and cooking.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The forest products industry is a large part of the economy in both developed and developing countries. Short-term economic gains made by conversion of forest to agriculture, or <a href="/wiki/Over-exploitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-exploitation">over-exploitation</a> of wood products, typically leads to loss of long-term income and long-term biological productivity. <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and many other regions have experienced lower revenue because of declining timber harvests. Illegal logging causes billions of dollars of losses to national economies annually.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>The new procedures to get amounts of wood are causing more harm to the economy and overpower the amount of money spent by people employed in logging.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> According to a study, "in most areas studied, the various ventures that prompted deforestation rarely generated more than US$5 for every ton of carbon they released and frequently returned far less than US$1". The price on the European market for an offset tied to a one-ton reduction in carbon is 23 <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> (about US$35).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rapidly growing economies also have an effect on deforestation. Most pressure will come from the world's developing countries, which have the fastest-growing populations and most rapid economic (industrial) growth.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-78">[78]</a></sup> In 1995, economic growth in developing countries reached nearly 6%, compared with the 2% growth rate for developed countries.”<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-78">[78]</a></sup> As our human population grows, new homes, communities, and expansions of cities will occur. Connecting all of the new expansions will be roads, a very important part in our daily life. Rural roads promote economic development but also facilitate deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-78">[78]</a></sup> About 90% of the deforestation has occurred within 100 km of roads in most parts of the Amazon.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> is one of the largest importer of products made from illegal deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forest_transition_theory">Forest transition theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Forest transition theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Forest_transition_theory.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bf/Forest_transition_theory.jpg/220px-Forest_transition_theory.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bf/Forest_transition_theory.jpg/330px-Forest_transition_theory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/bf/Forest_transition_theory.jpg 2x" data-file-width="414" data-file-height="274" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Forest_transition_theory.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The forest transition and historical baselines.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The forest area change may follow a pattern suggested by the <a href="/wiki/Forest_transition" title="Forest transition">forest transition</a> (FT) theory,<sup id="cite_ref-MeyfroidtLambin2011_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MeyfroidtLambin2011-82">[82]</a></sup> whereby at early stages in its development a country is characterized by high forest cover and low deforestation rates (HFLD countries).<sup id="cite_ref-rud_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rud-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Then deforestation rates accelerate (HFHD, high forest cover – high deforestation rate), and forest cover is reduced (LFHD, low forest cover – high deforestation rate), before the deforestation rate slows (LFLD, low forest cover – low deforestation rate), after which forest cover stabilizes and eventually starts recovering. FT is not a “law of nature,” and the pattern is influenced by national context (for example, human population density, stage of development, structure of the economy), global economic forces, and government policies. A country may reach very low levels of forest cover before it stabilizes, or it might through good policies be able to “bridge” the forest transition.</p>
<p>FT depicts a broad trend, and an extrapolation of historical rates therefore tends to underestimate future BAU deforestation for counties at the early stages in the transition (HFLD), while it tends to overestimate BAU deforestation for countries at the later stages (LFHD and LFLD).</p>
<p>Countries with high forest cover can be expected to be at early stages of the FT. GDP per capita captures the stage in a country’s economic development, which is linked to the pattern of natural resource use, including forests. The choice of forest cover and GDP per capita also fits well with the two key scenarios in the FT:</p>
<p>(i) a forest scarcity path, where forest scarcity triggers forces (for example, higher prices of forest products) that lead to forest cover stabilization; and</p>
<p>(ii) an economic development path, where new and better off-farm employment opportunities associated with economic growth (= increasing GDP per capita) reduce profitability of frontier agriculture and slows deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-rud_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rud-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_causes">Historical causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Historical causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_environmental_history" title="Timeline of environmental history">Timeline of environmental history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous_Rainforest_Collapse" class="mw-redirect" title="Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse">Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse</a><sup id="cite_ref-Benton_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benton-6">[6]</a></sup> was an event that occurred 300 million years ago. Climate change devastated tropical rainforests causing the extinction of many plant and animal species. The change was abrupt, specifically, at this time climate became cooler and drier, conditions that are not favourable to the growth of rainforests and much of the biodiversity within them. Rainforests were fragmented forming shrinking 'islands' further and further apart. This sudden collapse affected several large groups, effects on amphibians were particularly devastating, while reptiles fared better, being ecologically adapted to the drier conditions that followed.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/220px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/330px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/440px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An array of Neolithic artifacts, including bracelets, axe heads, chisels, and polishing tools.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rainforests once covered 14% of the earth's land surface; now they cover a mere 6% and experts estimate that the last remaining rainforests could be consumed in less than 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Small scale deforestation was practiced by some societies for tens of thousands of years before the beginnings of civilization.<sup id="cite_ref-FutureEaters_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FutureEaters-84">[84]</a></sup> The first evidence of deforestation appears in the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesolithic period">Mesolithic period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> It was probably used to convert closed forests into more open ecosystems favourable to game animals.<sup id="cite_ref-FutureEaters_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FutureEaters-84">[84]</a></sup> With the advent of agriculture, larger areas began to be deforested, and fire became the prime tool to clear land for crops. In Europe there is little solid evidence before 7000 BC. Mesolithic <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">foragers</a> used fire to create openings for <a href="/wiki/Red_deer" title="Red deer">red deer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a>. In Great Britain, shade-tolerant species such as <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ash_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="Ash tree">ash</a> are replaced in the <a href="/wiki/Palynology" title="Palynology">pollen record</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hazel" title="Hazel">hazels</a>, brambles, grasses and nettles. Removal of the forests led to decreased <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>, resulting in the formation of upland <a href="/wiki/Peat_bog" class="mw-redirect" title="Peat bog">peat bogs</a>. Widespread decrease in <a href="/wiki/Elm" title="Elm">elm</a> <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> across Europe between 8400–8300 BC and 7200–7000 BC, starting in southern Europe and gradually moving north to Great Britain, may represent land clearing by fire at the onset of <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> agriculture.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Neolithic period">Neolithic period</a> saw extensive deforestation for <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">farming land</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Stone axes were being made from about 3000 BC not just from flint, but from a wide variety of hard rocks from across Britain and North America as well. They include the noted <a href="/wiki/Langdale_axe_industry" title="Langdale axe industry">Langdale axe industry</a> in the <a href="/wiki/English_Lake_District" class="mw-redirect" title="English Lake District">English Lake District</a>, quarries developed at <a href="/wiki/Penmaenmawr" title="Penmaenmawr">Penmaenmawr</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a> and numerous other locations. Rough-outs were made locally near the quarries, and some were polished locally to give a fine finish. This step not only increased the <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_strength" class="mw-redirect" title="Mechanical strength">mechanical strength</a> of the axe, but also made penetration of wood easier. <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">Flint</a> was still used from sources such as <a href="/wiki/Grimes_Graves" class="mw-redirect" title="Grimes Graves">Grimes Graves</a> but from many other mines across Europe.</p>
<p>Evidence of deforestation has been found in <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>; for example the environs of the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Knossos" class="mw-redirect" title="Palace of Knossos">Palace of Knossos</a> were severely deforested in the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-industrial_history">Pre-industrial history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Pre-industrial history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rano_Raraku_quarry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Rano_Raraku_quarry.jpg/220px-Rano_Raraku_quarry.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Rano_Raraku_quarry.jpg/330px-Rano_Raraku_quarry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Rano_Raraku_quarry.jpg/440px-Rano_Raraku_quarry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rano_Raraku_quarry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Easter Island, deforested. According to <a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">Jared Diamond</a>: "Among past societies faced with the prospect of ruinous deforestation, Easter Island and Mangareva chiefs succumbed to their immediate concerns, but Tokugawa shoguns, Inca emperors, New Guinea highlanders, and 16th century German landowners adopted a long view and reafforested."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout most of history, humans were hunter gatherers who hunted within forests. In most areas, such as the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_rainforest" title="Amazon rainforest">Amazon</a>, the tropics, Central America, and the Caribbean,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> only after shortages of wood and other forest products occur are policies implemented to ensure forest resources are used in a sustainable manner.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>, Tjeered van Andel and co-writers<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> summarized three regional studies of historic erosion and alluviation and found that, wherever adequate evidence exists, a major phase of erosion follows, by about 500-1,000 years the introduction of farming in the various regions of Greece, ranging from the later Neolithic to the Early Bronze Age. The thousand years following the mid-first millennium BC saw serious, intermittent pulses of soil erosion in numerous places. The historic <a href="/wiki/Silting" class="mw-redirect" title="Silting">silting</a> of ports along the southern coasts of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> (<i>e.g.</i> <a href="/wiki/Clarus" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarus">Clarus</a>, and the examples of <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Priene" title="Priene">Priene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, where harbors had to be abandoned because of the silt deposited by the Meander) and in coastal <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> during the last centuries BC.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a> has suffered from heavy <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a> in recent centuries, aggravated by agriculture and deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">Jared Diamond</a> gives an extensive look into the collapse of the ancient Easter Islanders in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Collapse_(book)" class="mw-redirect" title="Collapse (book)">Collapse</a></i>. The disappearance of the island's trees seems to coincide with a decline of its civilization around the 17th and 18th century. He attributed the collapse to deforestation and over-exploitation of all resources.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The famous silting up of the harbor for <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a>, which moved port commerce to <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a>, also followed a period of increased settlement growth (and apparently of deforestation) in the upper river basins. In early medieval <a href="/wiki/Riez" title="Riez">Riez</a> in upper <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>, alluvial silt from two small rivers raised the riverbeds and widened the floodplain, which slowly buried the Roman settlement in alluvium and gradually moved new construction to higher ground; concurrently the headwater valleys above Riez were being opened to pasturage.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A typical <a href="/wiki/Progress_trap" title="Progress trap">progress trap</a> was that cities were often built in a forested area, which would provide wood for some industry (for example, construction, shipbuilding, pottery). When deforestation occurs without proper replanting, however; local wood supplies become difficult to obtain near enough to remain competitive, leading to the city's abandonment, as happened repeatedly in Ancient <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. Because of fuel needs, mining and metallurgy often led to deforestation and city abandonment.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>With most of the population remaining active in (or indirectly dependent on) the agricultural sector, the main pressure in most areas remained land clearing for crop and cattle farming. Enough wild green was usually left standing (and partially used, for example, to collect firewood, timber and fruits, or to graze pigs) for wildlife to remain viable. The elite's (nobility and higher clergy) protection of their own hunting privileges and game often protected significant woodlands.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Major parts in the spread (and thus more durable growth) of the population were played by monastical 'pioneering' (especially by the <a href="/wiki/Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">Commercial</a> orders) and some <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> lords' recruiting farmers to settle (and become tax payers) by offering relatively good legal and fiscal conditions. Even when speculators sought to encourage towns, settlers needed an agricultural belt around or sometimes within defensive walls. When populations were quickly decreased by causes such as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> or devastating warfare (for example, <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mongol" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongol">Mongol</a> hordes in eastern and central Europe, <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> in Germany), this could lead to settlements being abandoned. The land was reclaimed by nature, but the <a href="/wiki/Secondary_forest" title="Secondary forest">secondary forests</a> usually lacked the original <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rugendas_-_Defrichement_d_une_Foret.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Rugendas_-_Defrichement_d_une_Foret.jpg/220px-Rugendas_-_Defrichement_d_une_Foret.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Rugendas_-_Defrichement_d_une_Foret.jpg/330px-Rugendas_-_Defrichement_d_une_Foret.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Rugendas_-_Defrichement_d_une_Foret.jpg/440px-Rugendas_-_Defrichement_d_une_Foret.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="614" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rugendas_-_Defrichement_d_une_Foret.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Deforestation of Brazil's <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Forest" title="Atlantic Forest">Atlantic Forest</a> c.1820–1825</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1100 to 1500 AD, significant deforestation took place in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">expanding human population</a>. The large-scale building of wooden sailing ships by European (coastal) naval owners since the 15th century for exploration, <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a>–and other trade on the high seas consumed many forest resources. <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">Piracy</a> also contributed to the over harvesting of forests, as in Spain. This led to a weakening of the domestic economy after Columbus' discovery of America, as the economy became dependent on colonial activities (plundering, mining, cattle, plantations, trade, etc.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In <i>Changes in the Land</i> (1983), <a href="/wiki/William_Cronon" title="William Cronon">William Cronon</a> analyzed and documented 17th-century English colonists' reports of increased seasonal flooding in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> during the period when new settlers initially cleared the forests for agriculture. They believed flooding was linked to widespread forest clearing upstream.</p>
<p>The massive use of <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> on an industrial scale in <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern Europe">Early Modern Europe</a> was a new type of consumption of western forests; even in Stuart England, the relatively primitive production of charcoal has already reached an impressive level. Stuart England was so widely deforested that it depended on the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_region" title="Baltic region">Baltic</a> trade for ship timbers, and looked to the untapped forests of <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> to supply the need. Each of Nelson's <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> war ships at Trafalgar (1805) required 6,000 mature oaks for its construction. In France, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Colbert" title="Jean-Baptiste Colbert">Colbert</a> planted <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> forests to supply the French navy in the future. When the oak plantations matured in the mid-19th century, the masts were no longer required because shipping had changed.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Norman_F._Cantor" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman F. Cantor">Norman F. Cantor</a>'s summary of the effects of late medieval deforestation applies equally well to Early Modern Europe:<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Europeans had lived in the midst of vast forests throughout the earlier medieval centuries. After 1250 they became so skilled at deforestation that by 1500 they were running short of wood for heating and cooking. They were faced with a nutritional decline because of the elimination of the generous supply of wild game that had inhabited the now-disappearing forests, which throughout medieval times had provided the staple of their carnivorous high-protein diet. By 1500 Europe was on the edge of a fuel and nutritional disaster [from] which it was saved in the sixteenth century only by the burning of soft coal and the cultivation of potatoes and maize.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_era">Industrial era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Industrial era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the 19th century, introduction of <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamboats</a> in the United States was the cause of deforestation of banks of major rivers, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>, with increased and more severe flooding one of the environmental results. The steamboat crews cut wood every day from the riverbanks to fuel the steam engines. Between <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a> and the confluence with the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> to the south, the Mississippi became more wide and shallow, and changed its channel laterally. Attempts to improve navigation by the use of <a href="/wiki/Snagboat" title="Snagboat">snag pullers</a> often resulted in crews' clearing large trees 100 to 200 feet (61&#160;m) back from the banks. Several French colonial towns of the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Country" title="Illinois Country">Illinois Country</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Kaskaskia,_Illinois" title="Kaskaskia, Illinois">Kaskaskia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cahokia,_Illinois" title="Cahokia, Illinois">Cahokia</a> and St. Philippe, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> were flooded and abandoned in the late 19th century, with a loss to the cultural record of their <a href="/wiki/Archeology" class="mw-redirect" title="Archeology">archeology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The wholescale clearance of woodland to create agricultural land can be seen in many parts of the world, such as the <a href="/wiki/Central_forest-grasslands_transition" title="Central forest-grasslands transition">Central forest-grasslands transition</a> and other areas of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. Specific parallels are seen in the 20th-century deforestation occurring in many developing nations.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rates_of_deforestation">Rates of deforestation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rates of deforestation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fires_and_Deforestation_on_the_Amazon_Frontier,_Rondonia,_Brazil_-_August_12,_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Fires_and_Deforestation_on_the_Amazon_Frontier%2C_Rondonia%2C_Brazil_-_August_12%2C_2007.jpg/220px-Fires_and_Deforestation_on_the_Amazon_Frontier%2C_Rondonia%2C_Brazil_-_August_12%2C_2007.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Fires_and_Deforestation_on_the_Amazon_Frontier%2C_Rondonia%2C_Brazil_-_August_12%2C_2007.jpg/330px-Fires_and_Deforestation_on_the_Amazon_Frontier%2C_Rondonia%2C_Brazil_-_August_12%2C_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Fires_and_Deforestation_on_the_Amazon_Frontier%2C_Rondonia%2C_Brazil_-_August_12%2C_2007.jpg/440px-Fires_and_Deforestation_on_the_Amazon_Frontier%2C_Rondonia%2C_Brazil_-_August_12%2C_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3836" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fires_and_Deforestation_on_the_Amazon_Frontier,_Rondonia,_Brazil_-_August_12,_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">Slash-and-burn</a> farming in the state of Rondônia, western Brazil</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Global deforestation<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> sharply accelerated around 1852.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> It has been estimated that about half of the Earth's mature <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a>—between 7.5 million and 8 million km<sup>2</sup> (2.9 million to 3 million sq mi) of the original 15 million to 16 million km<sup>2</sup> (5.8 million to 6.2 million sq mi) that until 1947 covered the planet<sup id="cite_ref-worldbook_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldbook-101">[101]</a></sup>—have now been destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-Rainforest_Facts_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rainforest_Facts-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-102">[102]</a></sup> Some scientists have predicted that unless significant measures (such as seeking out and protecting old growth forests that have not been disturbed)<sup id="cite_ref-worldbook_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldbook-101">[101]</a></sup> are taken on a worldwide basis, by 2030 there will only be 10% remaining,<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-102">[102]</a></sup> with another 10% <a href="/wiki/Degraded_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Degraded forest">in a degraded condition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-99">[99]</a></sup> 80% will have been lost, and with them hundreds of thousands of irreplaceable species.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-99">[99]</a></sup> Some cartographers have attempted to illustrate the sheer scale of deforestation by country using a <a href="/wiki/Cartogram" title="Cartogram">cartogram</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Estimates vary widely as to the extent of tropical deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Scientists estimate that one fifth of the world's tropical rainforest was destroyed between 1960 and 1990.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They claim that that rainforests 60 years ago covered 14%<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of the world's land surface, now only cover 5–7%, and that all tropical forests will be gone by the middle of the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-envsci-encyc-104_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-envsci-encyc-104-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2002 analysis of satellite imagery suggested that the rate of deforestation in the humid tropics (approximately 5.8 million hectares per year) was roughly 23% lower than the most commonly quoted rates.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Conversely, a newer analysis of satellite images reveals that <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_of_the_Amazon_rainforest" title="Deforestation of the Amazon rainforest">deforestation of the Amazon rainforest</a> is twice as fast as scientists previously estimated.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some have argued that deforestation trends may follow a <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Kuznets_Curve" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental Kuznets Curve">Kuznets curve</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> which if true would nonetheless fail to eliminate the risk of irreversible loss of non-economic forest values (for example, the extinction of species).<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haiti_deforestation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Haiti_deforestation.jpg/220px-Haiti_deforestation.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Haiti_deforestation.jpg/330px-Haiti_deforestation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Haiti_deforestation.jpg/440px-Haiti_deforestation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haiti_deforestation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Satellite image of <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>'s border with the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> (right) shows the amount of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Haiti" title="Deforestation in Haiti">deforestation on the Haitian side</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A 2005 report by the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO) estimated that although the Earth's total forest area continued to decrease at about 13 million hectares per year, the global rate of deforestation has recently been slowing.<sup id="cite_ref-pantropical_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pantropical-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The 2016 report by the FAO<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> reports from 2010 to 2015 there was a worldwide decrease in forest area of 3.3 million ha per year. During this five-year period, the biggest forest area loss occurred in the tropics, particularly in South America and Africa. Per capita forest area decline was also greatest in the tropics and subtropics but is occurring in every climatic domain (except in the temperate) as populations increase.</p>
<p>Others claim that rainforests are being destroyed at an ever-quickening pace.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The London-based <a href="/wiki/Rainforest_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Rainforest Foundation">Rainforest Foundation</a> notes that "the UN figure is based on a definition of forest as being an area with as little as 10% actual tree cover, which would therefore include areas that are actually savannah-like ecosystems and badly damaged forests."<sup id="cite_ref-Butler_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-117">[117]</a></sup> Other critics of the FAO data point out that they do not distinguish between forest types,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> and that they are based largely on reporting from forestry departments of individual countries,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> which do not take into account unofficial activities like illegal logging.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite these uncertainties, there is agreement that destruction of rainforests remains a significant environmental problem. Up to 90% of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>'s coastal rainforests have disappeared since 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, about 88% of the rainforests have been lost.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Much of what remains of the world's rainforests is in the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_basin" title="Amazon basin">Amazon basin</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon Rainforest</a> covers approximately 4 million square kilometres.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The regions with the highest tropical deforestation rate between 2000 and 2005 were <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>—which lost 1.3% of its forests each year—and tropical Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Butler_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-117">[117]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>, two-thirds of lowland tropical forests have been turned into pasture since 1950 and 40% of all the rainforests have been lost in the last 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-ru.org_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ru.org-124">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> has lost 90–95% of its <a href="/wiki/Mata_Atl%C3%A2ntica" class="mw-redirect" title="Mata Atlântica">Mata Atlântica</a> forest.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> was losing its natural semi humid forests in the country’s western regions at a rate of 15.000 hectares at a randomly studied 2-month period in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Paraguay’s parliament refused in 2009 to pass a law that would have stopped cutting of natural forests altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Deforestation_around_Pakke_Tiger_Reserve,_India.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Deforestation_around_Pakke_Tiger_Reserve%2C_India.JPG/200px-Deforestation_around_Pakke_Tiger_Reserve%2C_India.JPG" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Deforestation_around_Pakke_Tiger_Reserve%2C_India.JPG/300px-Deforestation_around_Pakke_Tiger_Reserve%2C_India.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Deforestation_around_Pakke_Tiger_Reserve%2C_India.JPG/400px-Deforestation_around_Pakke_Tiger_Reserve%2C_India.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Deforestation_around_Pakke_Tiger_Reserve,_India.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Deforestation around Pakke Tiger Reserve, India</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> has lost 90% of its eastern rainforests.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> As of 2007, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Haiti" title="Deforestation in Haiti">less than 50% of Haiti's forests remained</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Indonesia" title="Deforestation in Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Malaysia" title="Deforestation in Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, China, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Sri_Lanka" title="Deforestation in Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>, have lost large areas of their rainforest.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Several countries, notably <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Brazil" title="Deforestation in Brazil">Brazil</a>, have declared their deforestation a national emergency.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregion</a> project catalogues habitat types throughout the world, including habitat loss such as deforestation, showing for example that even in the rich forests of parts of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Continental_Canadian_forests" title="Mid-Continental Canadian forests">Mid-Continental Canadian forests</a> of the prairie provinces half of the forest cover has been lost or altered.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions">Regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_by_region" title="Deforestation by region">Deforestation by region</a></div>
<p>Rates of deforestation vary around the world.</p>
<p>In 2011 <a href="/wiki/Conservation_International" title="Conservation International">Conservation International</a> listed the top 10 most endangered forests, characterized by having all lost 90% or more of their original <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a>, and each harboring at least 1500 <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> plant species (species found nowhere else in the world).<sup id="cite_ref-ci2011_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ci2011-135">[135]</a></sup></p>

<td>Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests<br />
<a href="/wiki/Montane_grasslands_and_shrublands" title="Montane grasslands and shrublands">Montane grasslands and shrublands</a></td>
<td>Forests scattered along the eastern edge of Africa, from <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> in the north to <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
</dd>
</dl>

<dd>
<dl>
<dd>Table source:<sup id="cite_ref-ci2011_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ci2011-135">[135]</a></sup></dd>
