</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Control">Control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_emissions">Reducing emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Reducing emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reducing_emissions_from_deforestation_and_forest_degradation" title="Reducing emissions from deforestation and forest degradation">Reducing emissions from deforestation and forest degradation</a></div>
<p>Main international organizations including the United Nations and the World Bank, have begun to develop programs aimed at curbing deforestation. The blanket term <a href="/wiki/Reducing_emissions_from_deforestation_and_forest_degradation" title="Reducing emissions from deforestation and forest degradation">Reducing Emissions from Deforestation and Forest Degradation</a> (REDD) describes these sorts of programs, which use direct monetary or other incentives to encourage developing countries to limit and/or roll back deforestation. Funding has been an issue, but at the <a href="/wiki/UN_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Framework Convention on Climate Change">UN Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) Conference of the Parties-15 (COP-15) in Copenhagen in December 2009, an accord was reached with a collective commitment by developed countries for new and additional resources, including forestry and investments through international institutions, that will approach USD 30 billion for the period 2010–2012.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Significant work is underway on tools for use in monitoring developing country adherence to their agreed REDD targets. These tools, which rely on remote forest monitoring using satellite imagery and other data sources, include the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Global_Development" title="Center for Global Development">Center for Global Development</a>'s FORMA (Forest Monitoring for Action) initiative <sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Group_on_Earth_Observations" title="Group on Earth Observations">Group on Earth Observations</a>' Forest Carbon Tracking Portal.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Methodological guidance for forest monitoring was also emphasized at COP-15.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The environmental organization <a href="/wiki/Avoided_Deforestation_Partners" title="Avoided Deforestation Partners">Avoided Deforestation Partners</a> leads the campaign for development of REDD through funding from the U.S. government.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization_of_the_United_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations">Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations</a> and partners launched Open Foris – a set of open-source software tools that assist countries in gathering, producing and disseminating information on the state of forest resources.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> The tools support the inventory lifecycle, from needs assessment, design, planning, field data collection and management, estimation analysis, and dissemination. Remote sensing image processing tools are included, as well as tools for international reporting for <a href="/wiki/Reducing_emissions_from_deforestation_and_forest_degradation" title="Reducing emissions from deforestation and forest degradation">Reducing emissions from deforestation and forest degradation</a> (REDD) and MRV<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and FAO's <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.fao.org/forestry/fra/en/">Global Forest Resource Assessments</a>.</p>
<p>In evaluating implications of overall emissions reductions, countries of greatest concern are those categorized as High Forest Cover with High Rates of Deforestation (HFHD) and Low Forest Cover with High Rates of Deforestation (LFHD). Afghanistan, Benin, Botswana, Burma, Burundi, Cameroon, Chad, Ecuador, El Salvador, Ethiopia, Ghana, Guatemala, Guinea, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Haiti" title="Deforestation in Haiti">Haiti</a>, Honduras, Indonesia, Liberia, Malawi, Mali, Mauritania, Mongolia, Namibia, Nepal, Nicaragua, Niger, Nigeria, Pakistan, Paraguay, Philippines, Senegal, Sierra Leone, Sri Lanka, Sudan, Togo, Uganda, United Republic of Tanzania, Zimbabwe are listed as having Low Forest Cover with High Rates of Deforestation (LFHD). Brazil, Cambodia, Democratic Peoples Republic of Korea, Equatorial Guinea, Malaysia, Solomon Islands, Timor-Leste, Venezuela, Zambia are listed as High Forest Cover with High Rates of Deforestation (HFHD).<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Payments_for_conserving_forests">Payments for conserving forests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Payments for conserving forests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Bolivia, deforestation in upper river basins has caused environmental problems, including soil erosion and declining water quality. An innovative project to try and remedy this situation involves landholders in upstream areas being paid by downstream water users to conserve forests. The landholders receive US$20 to conserve the trees, avoid polluting livestock practices, and enhance the biodiversity and forest carbon on their land. They also receive US$30, which purchases a beehive, to compensate for conservation for two hectares of water-sustaining forest for five years. Honey revenue per hectare of forest is US$5 per year, so within five years, the landholder has sold US$50 of honey.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The project is being conducted by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Fundaci%C3%B3n_Natura_Bolivia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Fundación Natura Bolivia (page does not exist)">Fundación Natura Bolivia</a> and Rare Conservation, with support from the <a href="/wiki/Climate_%26_Development_Knowledge_Network" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate &amp; Development Knowledge Network">Climate &amp; Development Knowledge Network</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_rights">Land rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Land rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/250px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/375px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/500px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2234" data-file-height="1490" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Transferring <a href="/wiki/Land_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Land rights">land rights</a> to indigenous inhabitants is argued to efficiently conserve forests.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Transferring rights over land from public domain to its indigenous inhabitants is argued to be a cost effective strategy to conserve forests.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian1_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian1-154">[154]</a></sup> This includes the protection of such rights entitled in existing laws, such as India’s <a href="/wiki/The_Scheduled_Tribes_and_Other_Traditional_Forest_Dwellers_(Recognition_of_Forest_Rights)_Act,_2006" title="The Scheduled Tribes and Other Traditional Forest Dwellers (Recognition of Forest Rights) Act, 2006">Forest Rights Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian1_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian1-154">[154]</a></sup> The transferring of such rights in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, perhaps the largest <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a> in modern times, has been argued to have increased forest cover.<sup id="cite_ref-rightsandresources_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rightsandresources-155">[155]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, forested areas given tenure to indigenous groups have even lower rates of clearing than <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rightsandresources_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rightsandresources-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farming">Farming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Farming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>New methods are being developed to farm more intensively, such as high-yield <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> crops, <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_building" title="Autonomous building">autonomous building</a> gardens, and <a href="/wiki/Hydroponic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroponic">hydroponics</a>. These methods are often dependent on chemical inputs to maintain necessary yields. In cyclic <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, cattle are grazed on farm land that is resting and rejuvenating. Cyclic agriculture actually increases the fertility of the soil. Intensive farming can also decrease soil nutrients by consuming at an accelerated rate the trace minerals needed for crop growth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>The most promising approach, however, is the concept of <a href="/wiki/Forest_gardening" title="Forest gardening">food forests</a> in <a href="/wiki/Permaculture" title="Permaculture">permaculture</a>, which consists of agroforestal systems carefully designed to mimic natural forests, with an emphasis on plant and animal species of interest for food, timber and other uses. These systems have low dependence on <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agro-chemicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Agro-chemicals">agro-chemicals</a>, are highly self-maintaining, highly productive, and with strong positive impact on soil and water quality, and <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monitoring_deforestation">Monitoring deforestation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Monitoring deforestation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are multiple methods that are appropriate and reliable for reducing and monitoring deforestation. One method is the “visual interpretation of aerial photos or satellite imagery that is labor-intensive but does not require high-level training in computer image processing or extensive computational resources”.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-79">[79]</a></sup> Another method includes hot-spot analysis (that is, locations of rapid change) using expert opinion or coarse resolution satellite data to identify locations for detailed digital analysis with high resolution satellite images.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-79">[79]</a></sup> Deforestation is typically assessed by quantifying the amount of area deforested, measured at the present time. From an environmental point of view, quantifying the damage and its possible consequences is a more important task, while conservation efforts are more focused on forested land protection and development of land-use alternatives to avoid continued deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-79">[79]</a></sup> Deforestation rate and total area deforested, have been widely used for monitoring deforestation in many regions, including the Brazilian Amazon deforestation monitoring by INPE.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-47">[47]</a></sup> A global satellite view is available.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forest_management">Forest management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Forest management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Efforts to stop or slow deforestation have been attempted for many centuries because it has long been known that deforestation can cause environmental damage sufficient in some cases to cause societies to collapse. In <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a>, paramount rulers developed policies designed to prevent conflicts between short-term gains from converting forest to farmland and long-term problems forest loss would cause,<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> while during the 17th and 18th centuries in <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa</a>, Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> the shoguns developed a highly sophisticated system of long-term planning to stop and even reverse deforestation of the preceding centuries through substituting timber by other products and more efficient use of land that had been farmed for many centuries. In 16th-century Germany, landowners also developed <a href="/wiki/Silviculture" title="Silviculture">silviculture</a> to deal with the problem of deforestation. However, these policies tend to be limited to environments with <i>good rainfall</i>, <i>no dry season</i> and <i>very young <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a></i> (through <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Glaciation" class="mw-redirect" title="Glaciation">glaciation</a>). This is because on older and less fertile soils trees grow too slowly for silviculture to be economic, whilst in areas with a strong dry season there is always a risk of forest fires destroying a tree crop before it matures.</p>
<p>In the areas where "<a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a>" is practiced, switching to "<a href="/wiki/Slash-and-char" title="Slash-and-char">slash-and-char</a>" would prevent the rapid deforestation and subsequent degradation of soils. The <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">biochar</a> thus created, given back to the soil, is not only a durable <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">carbon sequestration</a> method, but it also is an extremely beneficial <a href="/wiki/Soil_conditioner" title="Soil conditioner">amendment</a> to the soil. Mixed with <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> it brings the creation of <a href="/wiki/Terra_preta" title="Terra preta">terra preta</a>, one of the richest soils on the planet and the only one known to regenerate itself.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainable_practices">Sustainable practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sustainable practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bamboo_Feb09.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bamboo_Feb09.jpg/220px-Bamboo_Feb09.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bamboo_Feb09.jpg/330px-Bamboo_Feb09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bamboo_Feb09.jpg/440px-Bamboo_Feb09.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bamboo_Feb09.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bamboo is advocated as a more sustainable alternative for cutting down wood for fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-bamboo_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bamboo-160">[160]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Certification, as provided by global certification systems such as <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_the_Endorsement_of_Forest_Certification" title="Programme for the Endorsement of Forest Certification">Programme for the Endorsement of Forest Certification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forest_Stewardship_Council" title="Forest Stewardship Council">Forest Stewardship Council</a>, contributes to tackling deforestation by creating market demand for timber from sustainably managed forests. According to the United Nations Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO), "A major condition for the adoption of sustainable forest management is a demand for products that are produced sustainably and consumer willingness to pay for the higher costs entailed. Certification represents a shift from regulatory approaches to market incentives to promote sustainable forest management. By promoting the positive attributes of forest products from sustainably managed forests, certification focuses on the demand side of environmental conservation."<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rettet_den_Regenwald" title="Rettet den Regenwald">Rainforest Rescue</a> argues that the standards of organizations like FSC are too closely connected to <a href="/wiki/Timber_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Timber industry">timber industry</a> interests and therefore do not guarantee environmentally and socially responsible forest management. In reality, monitoring systems are inadequate and various cases of fraud have been documented worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some nations have taken steps to help increase the amount of trees on Earth. In 1981, China created National Tree Planting Day Forest and forest coverage had now reached 16.55% of China's land mass, as against only 12% two decades ago.<sup id="cite_ref-battling_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-battling-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>Using fuel from <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> rather than wood results in cleaner burning, and since bamboo matures much faster than wood, deforestation is reduced as supply can be replenished faster.<sup id="cite_ref-bamboo_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bamboo-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforestation">Reforestation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Reforestation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">Reforestation</a></div>
<p>In many parts of the world, especially in East Asian countries, reforestation and <a href="/wiki/Afforestation" title="Afforestation">afforestation</a> are increasing the area of forested lands.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The amount of woodland has increased in 22 of the world's 50 most forested nations. Asia as a whole gained 1 million <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectares</a> of forest between 2000 and 2005. Tropical forest in El Salvador expanded more than 20% between 1992 and 2001. Based on these trends, one study projects that global forest will increase by 10%—an area the size of India—by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-Owen_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, where large scale destruction of forests has occurred, the government has in the past required that every able-bodied citizen between the ages of 11 and 60 plant three to five trees per year or do the equivalent amount of work in other forest services. The government claims that at least 1 <a href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> trees have been planted in China every year since 1982. This is no longer required today, but 12 March of every year in China is the Planting Holiday. Also, it has introduced the <a href="/wiki/Green_Wall_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Wall of China">Green Wall of China</a> project, which aims to halt the expansion of the Gobi desert through the planting of trees. However, due to the large percentage of trees dying off after planting (up to 75%), the project is not very successful.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> There has been a 47-million-hectare increase in forest area in China since the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Owen_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-165">[165]</a></sup> The total number of trees amounted to be about 35 billion and 4.55% of China's land mass increased in forest coverage. The forest coverage was 12% two decades ago and now is 16.55%.<sup id="cite_ref-battling_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-battling-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>An ambitious proposal for China is the Aerially Delivered Re-forestation and Erosion Control System and the proposed Sahara Forest Project coupled with the <a href="/wiki/Seawater_Greenhouse" class="mw-redirect" title="Seawater Greenhouse">Seawater Greenhouse</a>.</p>
<p>In Western countries, increasing consumer demand for wood products that have been produced and harvested in a sustainable manner is causing forest landowners and forest industries to become increasingly accountable for their forest management and timber harvesting practices.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arbor_Day_Foundation" title="Arbor Day Foundation">Arbor Day Foundation</a>'s Rain Forest Rescue program is a charity that helps to prevent deforestation. The charity uses donated money to buy up and preserve rainforest land before the <a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">lumber</a> companies can buy it. The Arbor Day Foundation then protects the land from deforestation. This also locks in the way of life of the primitive tribes living on the forest land. Organizations such as <a href="/wiki/Community_Forestry_International" title="Community Forestry International">Community Forestry International</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cool_Earth" title="Cool Earth">Cool Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Nature_Conservancy" title="The Nature Conservancy">The Nature Conservancy</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conservation_International" title="Conservation International">Conservation International</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Conservation_Foundation" title="African Conservation Foundation">African Conservation Foundation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> also focus on preserving forest habitats. Greenpeace in particular has also mapped out the forests that are still intact<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> and published this information on the internet.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> World Resources Institute in turn has made a simpler thematic map<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> showing the amount of forests present just before the age of man (8000 years ago) and the current (reduced) levels of forest.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> These maps mark the amount of afforestation required to repair the damage caused by people.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forest_plantations">Forest plantations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Forest plantations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To meet the world's demand for wood, it has been suggested by forestry writers Botkins and <a href="/wiki/Sedjo" class="mw-redirect" title="Sedjo">Sedjo</a> that high-yielding forest <a href="/wiki/Plantations" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantations">plantations</a> are suitable. It has been calculated that plantations yielding 10 cubic meters per hectare annually could supply all the timber required for international trade on 5% of the world's existing forestland. By contrast, natural forests produce about 1–2 cubic meters per hectare; therefore, 5–10 times more forestland would be required to meet demand. Forester Chad Oliver has suggested a forest mosaic with high-yield forest lands interspersed with conservation land.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>Globally, planted forests increased from 4.1% to 7.0% of the total forest area between 1990 and 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Payn.2C_T._2015_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Payn.2C_T._2015-171">[171]</a></sup> Plantation forests made up 280 million ha in 2015, an increase of about 40 million ha in the last ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Globally, planted forests consist of about 18% exotic or introduced species while the rest are species native to the country where they are planted. In South America, Oceania, and East and Southern Africa, planted forests are dominated by introduced species: 88%, 75% and 65%, respectively. In North America, West and Central Asia, and Europe the proportions of introduced species in plantations are much lower at 1%, 3% and 8% of the total area planted, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Payn.2C_T._2015_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Payn.2C_T._2015-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the country of Senegal, on the western coast of Africa, a movement headed by youths has helped to plant over 6 million mangrove trees. The trees will protect local villages from storm damages and will provide a habitat for local wildlife. The project started in 2008, and already the Senegalese government has been asked to establish rules and regulations that would protect the new mangrove forests.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_context">Military context</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Military context">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_war" title="Environmental impact of war">Environmental impact of war</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Attack_on_bloody_ridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Attack_on_bloody_ridge.jpg/220px-Attack_on_bloody_ridge.jpg" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Attack_on_bloody_ridge.jpg/330px-Attack_on_bloody_ridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Attack_on_bloody_ridge.jpg/440px-Attack_on_bloody_ridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="594" data-file-height="331" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Attack_on_bloody_ridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
American <a href="/wiki/M4_Sherman" title="M4 Sherman">Sherman tanks</a> knocked out by Japanese artillery on Okinawa.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the preponderance of deforestation is due to demands for agricultural and urban use for the human population, there are some examples of military causes. One example of deliberate deforestation is that which took place in the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Allied_Occupation_Zones_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Allied Occupation Zones in Germany">zone of occupation</a> in Germany after World War II. Before the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, defeated Germany was still considered a potential future threat rather than potential future ally. To address this threat, attempts were made to <a href="/wiki/Industrial_plans_for_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial plans for Germany">lower German industrial potential</a>, of which forests were deemed an element. Sources in the U.S. government admitted that the purpose of this was that the "ultimate destruction of the war potential of German forests." As a consequence of the practice of clear-felling, deforestation resulted which could "be replaced only by long forestry development over perhaps a century."<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>Deforestation can also be one consequence of <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>. For example, in the 1945 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Okinawa" title="Battle of Okinawa">Battle of Okinawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombardment" title="Bombardment">bombardment</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Military_operation" title="Military operation">combat operations</a> reduced the lush tropical landscape into "a vast field of mud, lead, decay and maggots".<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Deforestation can also be an intentional tactic of <a href="/wiki/Military_forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Military forces">military forces</a>. <a href="/wiki/Defoliant" title="Defoliant">Defoliants</a> (like <a href="/wiki/Agent_Orange" title="Agent Orange">Agent Orange</a> or others) was used by the British in the <a href="/wiki/Malayan_Emergency" title="Malayan Emergency">Malayan Emergency</a>, and by the United States in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health_context">Public health context</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deforestation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Public health context">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Deforestation eliminates a great number of species of plants and animals which also often results in an increase in disease.<sup id="cite_ref-CHGE_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHGE-179">[179]</a></sup> Loss of native species allows new species to come to dominance. Often the destruction of predatory species can result in an increase in rodent populations. These are known to carry plagues. Additionally, erosion can produce pools of stagnant water that are perfect breeding grounds for mosquitos, well known vectors of <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henipavirus" title="Henipavirus">nipah virus</a>, and more.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Deforestation can also create a path for non-native species to flourish such as certain types of snails, which have been correlated with an increase in <a href="/wiki/Schistosomiasis" title="Schistosomiasis">schistosomiasis</a> cases.<sup id="cite_ref-CHGE_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHGE-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>Deforestation is occurring all over the world and has been coupled with an increase in the occurrence of disease outbreaks. In <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, thousands of acres of forest have been cleared for pig farms. This has resulted in an increase in the zoonosis the Nipah virus.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, deforestation has led to an increase in malaria cases which is now the leading cause of morbidity and mortality the country.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another pathway through which deforestation affects disease is the relocation and dispersion of disease-carrying hosts. This disease emergence pathway can be called “<a href="/wiki/Colonisation_(biology)" title="Colonisation (biology)">range expansion</a>,” whereby the host’s range (and thereby the range of pathogens) expands to new geographic areas.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Through deforestation, hosts and reservoir species are forced into neighboring habitats. Accompanying the reservoir species are pathogens that have the ability to find new hosts in previously unexposed regions. As these pathogens and species come into closer contact with humans, they are infected both directly and indirectly.</p>
<p>A catastrophic example of range expansion is the 1998 outbreak of Nipah Virus in Malaysia.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> For a number of years, deforestation, drought, and subsequent fires led to a dramatic geographic shift and density of <a href="/wiki/Megabat" title="Megabat">fruit bats</a>, a reservoir for Nipah virus.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> Deforestation reduced the available fruiting trees in the bats’ habitat, and they encroached on surrounding orchards which also happened to be the location of a large number of pigsties. The bats, through proximity spread the Nipah to pigs. While the virus infected the pigs, mortality was much lower than among humans, making the pigs a virulent host leading to the transmission of the virus to humans. This resulted in 265 reported cases of <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">encephalitis</a>, of which 105 resulted in death. This example provides an important lesson for the impact deforestation can have on human health.</p>
<p>Another example of range expansion due to deforestation and other <a href="/wiki/Anthropogenic_hazard" title="Anthropogenic hazard">anthropogenic</a> habitat impacts includes the <a href="/wiki/Capybara" title="Capybara">Capybara</a> rodent in <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> This rodent is the host of a number of <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonotic</a> diseases and, while there has not yet been a human-borne outbreak due to the movement of this rodent into new regions, it offers an example of how habitat destruction through deforestation and subsequent movements of species is occurring regularly.</p>
<p>A now well-developed theory is that the spread of <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> it is at least partially due deforestation. Rising populations created a food demand and with deforestation opening up new areas of the forest the hunters harvested a great deal of primate bushmeat, which is believed to be the origin of HIV.<sup id="cite_ref-CHGE_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHGE-179">[179]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The fear is that highly diverse habitats, such as tropical rainforest, are vanishing at a faster rate that is partly masked by the slower deforestation of less biodiverse, dry, open forests. Because of this omission, the most harmful impacts of deforestation (such as habitat loss) could be increasing despite a possible decline in the global rate of deforestation.</span></li>
